<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Go (game)</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">This article is about the board game. For other things named "Go", see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Go_(disambiguation)" title="Go (disambiguation)">Go (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em">
<caption>Go</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FloorGoban.JPG"><img alt="photograph of Go equipment with game in progress" data-file-height="1101" data-file-width="1137" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/FloorGoban.JPG/300px-FloorGoban.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/FloorGoban.JPG/450px-FloorGoban.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/FloorGoban.JPG/600px-FloorGoban.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div>Go is played on a grid of black lines (usually 19×19). Game pieces, called <i>stones</i>, are played on the lines' intersections.</div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Years active</th>
<td>prior to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhou_Dynasty" title="Zhou Dynasty">Zhou Dynasty</a> (1046–256 BCE) to present</td>
</tr>

<a href="/wiki/Abstract_strategy_game" title="Abstract strategy game">Abstract strategy game</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Players</th>
<td>2</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Age range</th>
<td>3+<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[nb 1]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Setup time</th>
<td>Minimal</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Playing time</th>
<td>Casual: 20–90 minutes<br/>
Tournament: 1–6 hours<sup class="reference" id="ref_note1"><a href="#endnote_note1">[a]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Random chance</th>
<td>None</td>
</tr>

</tr>

<p><b>Go</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant" xml:lang="zh-Hant">圍棋</span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans" xml:lang="zh-Hans">围棋</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Zh-w%C3%A9iq%C3%AD.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0f/Zh-w%C3%A9iq%C3%AD.ogg" title="Zh-wéiqí.ogg">wéiqí</a></span></span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">囲碁</span>; <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Japanese" title="Romanization of Japanese">rōmaji</a>: <i>igo<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[nb 2]</a></sup></i>; <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>: <span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">바둑</span>; <a href="/wiki/Revised_Romanization_of_Korean" title="Revised Romanization of Korean">romaja</a>: <i>baduk<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[nb 3]</a></sup></i>; literally: "encircling game") is an <a href="/wiki/Abstract_strategy_game" title="Abstract strategy game">abstract strategy</a> <a href="/wiki/Board_game" title="Board game">board game</a> for two players, in which the aim is to surround more territory than the opponent.</p>
<p>The game was invented in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Science_and_Technology_in_China" title="History of Science and Technology in China">ancient China</a> more than 2,500 years ago, and is thus the oldest board game continuously played today. It was considered one of the <a href="/wiki/Four_arts" title="Four arts">four essential arts</a> of the cultured <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristocratic" title="Aristocratic">aristocratic</a> Chinese scholar <a href="/wiki/Caste" title="Caste">caste</a> in antiquity. The earliest written reference to the game is generally recognized as the historical annal <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zuo_Zhuan" title="Zuo Zhuan">Zuo Zhuan</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Tso_Chuan_book_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Tso_Chuan_book-5">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[3]</a></sup> (c. 4th century BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chronology2_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-chronology2-7">[4]</a></sup> The modern game of Go as we know it was formalized in Japan in the 15th century CE.</p>
<p>Despite its relatively <a href="#Rules">simple rules</a>, Go is very complex, even more so than <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">chess</a>, and possesses <a href="/wiki/Go_and_mathematics" title="Go and mathematics">more possibilities than the total number of atoms in the visible universe</a>. Compared to chess, Go has both a larger board with more scope for play and longer games, and, on average, many more alternatives to consider per move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_piece_(board_game)" title="Game piece (board game)">playing pieces</a> are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_stone" title="Go stone">stones</a>. One player uses the white stones and the other, black. The players take turns placing the stones on the vacant intersections (named "points") of a <a href="/wiki/Go_(game)#Boards" title="Go (game)">board</a> with a 19×19 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grid_graph" title="Grid graph">grid</a> of lines. Beginners often play on smaller 9×9 and 13×13 boards,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews.2C_Charles_p.1_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews.2C_Charles_p.1-9">[6]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological" title="Archaeological">archaeological</a> evidence shows that the game was once played on a 17×17 grid. However, boards with a 19×19 grid had become standard by the time the game had reached what was then the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tributaries_of_Imperial_China" title="List of tributaries of Imperial China">Imperial Chinese Tributary State</a> of <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> in the 5th century CE and later to what was then the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tributaries_of_Imperial_China" title="List of tributaries of Imperial China">Imperial Chinese Tributary State</a> of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in the 7th century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The objective of go—as the translation of its name implies—is to fully surround a larger total area of the board than the opponent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Once placed on the board, stones may not be moved, but stones are removed from the board when "captured". Capture happens when a stone or group of stones is surrounded by opposing stones on all <a href="/wiki/Orthogonality" title="Orthogonality">orthogonally</a>-adjacent points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iwamoto.2C_p.22_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iwamoto.2C_p.22-12">[9]</a></sup> The game proceeds until neither player wishes to make another move; the game has no set ending conditions beyond this. When a game concludes, the territory is counted along with captured stones and <a href="/wiki/Komidashi" title="Komidashi">komi</a> (points added to the score of the player with the white stones as compensation for playing second) to determine the winner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[10]</a></sup> Games may also be terminated by resignation.</p>
<p>As of mid-2008, there were well over 40 million Go players worldwide, the overwhelming majority of them living in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[11]</a></sup> As of December 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Go_Federation" title="International Go Federation">International Go Federation</a> has a total of 75 member countries and four Association Membership organizations in multiple countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-igf-members_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-igf-members-15">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Go-board-animated.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="369" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Go-board-animated.gif/220px-Go-board-animated.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Go-board-animated.gif/330px-Go-board-animated.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Go-board-animated.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Go-board-animated.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first 60 moves of a Go game animated. This particular game quickly developed into a complicated fight in the lower left and bottom.(Click on the board to restart the play in a larger window.)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Go is an adversarial game with the objective of surrounding a larger total area of the board with one's stones than the opponent. As the game progresses, the players position stones on the board to map out formations and potential territories. Contests between opposing formations are often extremely complex and may result in the expansion, reduction, or wholesale capture and loss of formation stones.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Golibs.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="477" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Golibs.png/220px-Golibs.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Golibs.png/330px-Golibs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Golibs.png/440px-Golibs.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Golibs.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The four liberties (adjacent empty points) of a single black stone (A), as White reduces those liberties by one (B, C, and D). When Black has only one liberty left (D), that stone is "in atari".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[13]</a></sup> White may capture that stone (remove from board) with a play on its last liberty (at D-1).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A basic principle of Go is that a group of stones must have at least one "<a href="/wiki/List_of_Go_terms#Liberty" title="List of Go terms">liberty</a>" to remain on the board. A "liberty" is an open "point" (intersection) bordering the group. An enclosed liberty (or liberties) is called an "<a href="/wiki/List_of_Go_terms#Eyes" title="List of Go terms">eye</a>", <span id="unconditionally-alive"></span>and a group of stones with two or more eyes is said to be unconditionally "alive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup> Such groups cannot be captured, even if surrounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[15]</a></sup> A group with one eye or no eyes is "dead" and cannot resist eventual capture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The general strategy is to expand one's territory, attack the opponent's weak groups (groups that can be killed), and always stay mindful of the "<a href="/wiki/Life_and_death" title="Life and death">life status</a>" of one's own groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> The liberties of groups are countable. Situations where mutually opposing groups must capture each other or die are called capturing races, or <i><a href="/wiki/Capturing_race" title="Capturing race">semeai</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cho_Chikun.2C_p.69_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cho_Chikun.2C_p.69-22">[19]</a></sup> In a capturing race, the group with more liberties (and/or better "shape") will ultimately be able to capture the opponent's stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cho_Chikun.2C_p.69_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cho_Chikun.2C_p.69-22">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> Capturing races and the elements of life or death are the primary challenges of Go.</p>
<p>A player may pass on determining that the game offers no further opportunities for profitable play. The game ends when both players pass,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> and is then scored. For each player, the number of captured stones <i>komi</i> is subtracted from the number of controlled (surrounded) points in "liberties" or "eyes", and the player with the greater score wins the game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KGS:_Endgame_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-KGS:_Endgame-25">[22]</a></sup> Games may also be won by resignation of the opponent.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finer_points">Finer points</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Finer points">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the opening stages of the game, players typically establish positions (or "bases") in the corners and around the sides of the board. These bases help to quickly develop strong shapes which have many options for <i>life</i> (self-viability for a group of stones that prevents capture) and establish formations for potential territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup> Players usually start in the corners because establishing territory is easier with the aid of two edges of the board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup> Established corner opening sequences are called "<a href="/wiki/Joseki" title="Joseki">joseki</a>" and are often studied independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/List_of_Go_terms#Dame" title="List of Go terms">Dame</a>" are points that lie in-between the boundary walls of black and white, and as such are considered to be of no value to either side. "<a href="/wiki/List_of_Go_terms#Seki" title="List of Go terms">Seki</a>" are mutually alive pairs of white and black groups where neither has two eyes. A "ko" (Chinese and Japanese: <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">劫</span>) is a repeated-position shape that may be contested by making forcing moves elsewhere. After the forcing move is played, the ko may be "taken back" and returned to its original position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup> Some "ko fights" may be important and decide the life of a large group, while others may be worth just one or two points. Some ko fights are referred to as "picnic kos" when only one side has a lot to lose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> The Japanese call it a hanami (flower-viewing) ko.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Playing with others usually requires a knowledge of each player's strength, indicated by the player's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_rank" title="Go rank">rank</a> (30kyu→1kyu|1dan→6dan|1dan pro→9dan pro). A difference in rank may be compensated by a handicap—Black is allowed to place two or more stones on the board to compensate for White's greater strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup> There are different rule-sets (Japanese, Chinese, AGA, etc.), which are almost entirely equivalent, except for certain special-case positions.</p>
<p>Aside from the order of play (alternating moves, Black moves first or takes a handicap) and scoring rules, there are essentially only two rules in Go:</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Rule 1</b> (the rule of liberty) states that <i>every stone remaining on the board must have at least one open "point" (an intersection, called a "liberty") directly orthogonally adjacent (up, down, left, or right)</i>, <b>or</b> <i>must be part of a connected group that has at least one such open point ("liberty") next to it. Stones or groups of stones which lose their last liberty are removed from the board.</i></li>
<li><b>Rule 2</b> (<a href="#The_ko_rule">the "ko rule"</a>) states that <i>the stones on the board must never repeat a previous position of stones. Moves which would do so are forbidden, and thus only moves elsewhere on the board are permitted that turn.</i></li>
</ul>
<p>Almost all other information about how the game is played is a heuristic, meaning it is learned information about how the game is played, rather than a rule. Other rules are specialized, as they come about through different rule-sets, but the above two rules cover almost all of any played game.</p>
<p>Although there are some minor differences between rule sets used in different countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RulesComparison_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-RulesComparison-34">[31]</a></sup> most notably in Chinese and Japanese scoring rules,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup> these differences do not greatly affect the tactics and strategy of the game.</p>
<p>Except where noted, the basic rules presented here are valid independent of the scoring rules used. The scoring rules are explained separately. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_terms" title="Go terms">Go terms</a> for which there are no ready English equivalent are commonly called by their Japanese names.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rules">Rules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rules_of_Go" title="Rules of Go">Rules of Go</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_rules">Basic rules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Basic rules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Go_adjacent_stones.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="229" data-file-width="275" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Go_adjacent_stones.png/220px-Go_adjacent_stones.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/Go_adjacent_stones.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Go_adjacent_stones.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One black chain and two white chains, with their liberties marked with dots. Liberties are shared among all stones of a chain and can be counted. Here the black group has 5 liberties, while the two white chains have 4 liberties each.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Two players, <i>Black</i> and <i>White</i>, take turns placing a <i>stone</i> (game piece) of their own color on a vacant <i>point</i> (intersection) of the grid on a Go board. Black plays first. If there is a large difference in skill between the players, the weaker player typically uses Black and is allowed to place two or more stones on the board to compensate for the difference (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_handicaps" title="Go handicaps">Go handicaps</a>). The official grid comprises 19×19 lines, though the rules can be applied to any grid size. 13×13 and 9×9 boards are popular choices to teach beginners, or for playing quick games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> Once placed, a stone may not be moved to a different point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vertically and horizontally adjacent stones of the same color form a <i>chain</i> (also called a <i>string</i> or <i>group</i>) that cannot subsequently be subdivided and, in effect, becomes a single larger stone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> Only stones immediately connected to one another by the lines on the board create a chain; stones that are diagonally adjacent are not connected. Chains may be expanded by placing additional stones on adjacent intersections, and can be connected together by placing a stone on an intersection that is adjacent to two or more chains of the same color.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Go_capturing.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="418" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Go_capturing.png/220px-Go_capturing.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Go_capturing.png/330px-Go_capturing.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6e/Go_capturing.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Go_capturing.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
If White plays at A, the black chain loses its last liberty. It is captured and removed from the board.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A vacant point adjacent to a stone is called a <i>liberty</i> for that stone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[nb 4]</a></sup> Stones in a chain share their liberties. A chain of stones must have at least one liberty to remain on the board. When a chain is surrounded by opposing stones so that it has no liberties, it is <i>captured</i> and removed from the board.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ko_rule">Ko rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ko rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ko_fight" title="Ko fight">Ko fight</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:144px; font-size: 100%;">
<table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="border:0;border-collapse:collapse;padding:0;font-size:1px">

</tr>
<tr style="vertical-align:top;border:none">

<td><img alt="Go board diagram image.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Go_board_diagram_image.svg/28px-Go_board_diagram_image.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Go_board_diagram_image.svg/42px-Go_board_diagram_image.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Go_board_diagram_image.svg/56px-Go_board_diagram_image.svg.png 2x" width="28"/></td>
<td><img alt="Go r.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Go_r.svg/28px-Go_r.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Go_r.svg/42px-Go_r.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Go_r.svg/56px-Go_r.svg.png 2x" width="28"/></td>
</tr>
<tr style="vertical-align:top;border:none">
<td><img alt="Go dl.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Go_dl.svg/28px-Go_dl.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Go_dl.svg/42px-Go_dl.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Go_dl.svg/56px-Go_dl.svg.png 2x" width="28"/></td>
<td><img alt="Go d.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Go_d.svg/28px-Go_d.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Go_d.svg/42px-Go_d.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Go_d.svg/56px-Go_d.svg.png 2x" width="28"/></td>
<td><img alt="Go d.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Go_d.svg/28px-Go_d.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Go_d.svg/42px-Go_d.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Go_d.svg/56px-Go_d.svg.png 2x" width="28"/></td>
<td><img alt="Go d.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Go_d.svg/28px-Go_d.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Go_d.svg/42px-Go_d.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Go_d.svg/56px-Go_d.svg.png 2x" width="28"/></td>
<td><img alt="Go dr.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Go_dr.svg/28px-Go_dr.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Go_dr.svg/42px-Go_dr.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Go_dr.svg/56px-Go_dr.svg.png 2x" width="28"/></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="font-size: 88%;">
<p>An example of a situation in which the ko rule applies</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Players are not allowed to make a move that returns the game to the previous position. This rule, called the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ko_rule" title="Ko rule">ko rule</a></i>, prevents unending repetition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ko_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-ko-41">[37]</a></sup> As shown in the example pictured: Black has just played the stone marked <b>1</b>, capturing a white stone at the intersection marked with the red circle. If White were now allowed to play on the marked intersection, that move would capture the black stone marked <b>1</b> and recreate the situation before Black made the move marked <b>1</b>. Allowing this could result in an unending cycle of captures by both players. The ko rule therefore prohibits White from playing at the marked intersection immediately. Instead White must play elsewhere, or pass; Black can then end the ko by filling at the marked intersection, creating a five-stone black chain. If White wants to continue the ko (that specific repeating position), White tries to find a play elsewhere on the board that Black must answer; if Black answers, then White can retake the ko. A repetition of such exchanges is called a <i>ko fight</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ko_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ko-42">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the various rule-sets agree on the ko rule prohibiting returning the board to an <i>immediately</i> previous position, they deal in different ways with the relatively uncommon situation in which a player might recreate a past position that is further removed. See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rules_of_Go#Repetition" title="Rules of Go">Rules of Go: Repetition</a> for further information.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suicide">Suicide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Suicide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gochsurule.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="183" data-file-width="220" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/24/Gochsurule.png" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gochsurule.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Under normal rules, White cannot play at A because that point has no liberties. Under the Ing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ing_rules_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-ing_rules-43">[39]</a></sup> and New Zealand rules,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGA_rules_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-AGA_rules-44">[40]</a></sup> White may play A (a ko threat), leaving an empty three-space eye. Black naturally answers by playing at A, creating two eyes.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A player may not place a stone such that it or its group immediately has no liberties, unless doing so immediately deprives an enemy group of its final liberty. In the latter case, the enemy group is captured, leaving the new stone with at least one liberty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup> This rule is responsible for the all-important difference between one and two eyes: if a group with only one eye is fully surrounded on the outside, it can be killed with a stone placed in its single eye.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rules_of_go#Ing_rules" title="Rules of go">Ing</a> and New Zealand rules do not have this rule,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suicide_in_different_rules._46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suicide_in_different_rules.-46">[42]</a></sup> and there a player might destroy one of its own groups—"commit suicide". This play would only be useful in a limited set of situations involving a small interior space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Komi">Komi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Komi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Komidashi" title="Komidashi">Komidashi</a></div>
<p>Because Black has the advantage of playing the first move, the idea of awarding White some compensation came into being during the 20th century. This is called <a href="/wiki/Komidashi" title="Komidashi">komi</a>, which gives white a 6.5-point compensation under Japanese rules (number of points varies by rule set).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Komi_Change_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Komi_Change-48">[44]</a></sup> If there is a one-stone (rank) difference in strength between players, the stronger player takes White, and White may receive only a 0.5-point komi, to break a possible tie ("jigo"). In handicap games with two or more handicap stones, White may also take a 0.5-point komi to break a tie but it is more common that there is no komi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gobase.org_Komi_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gobase.org_Komi-49">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scoring_rules">Scoring rules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Scoring rules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gofin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="475" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Gofin.png/220px-Gofin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Gofin.png/330px-Gofin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Gofin.png/440px-Gofin.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gofin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A simplified game at its end. Black's territory (A) + (C) and prisoners (D) is counted and compared to White's territory (B) only (no prisoners). In this example, both Black and White attempted to invade and live (C and D groups) to reduce the other's total territory. Only Black's invading group (C) was successful in living, as White's group (D) was killed with a black stone at (E). The points in the middle (F) are "dame", meaning they belong to neither player.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Two general types of scoring system are used, and players determine which to use before play. Both systems almost always give the same result. <i>Territory scoring</i> counts the number of empty points a player's stones surround, together with the number of stones the player captured. <i>Area scoring</i> counts the number of points a player's stones occupy and surround. It is associated with contemporary Chinese play and was probably established there during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> in the 15th or 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>After both players have passed consecutively, the stones that are still on the board but unable to avoid capture, called <i>dead</i> stones, are removed.</p>
<p>'<b>Area scoring (including Chinese):'</b> A player's score is the number of stones that the player has on the board, plus the number of empty intersections surrounded by that player's stones.</p>
<p><b>Territory scoring (including Japanese and Korean):</b> In the course of the game, each player retains the stones they capture, termed <i>prisoners</i>. Any dead stones removed at the end of the game become prisoners. The score is the number of empty points enclosed by a player's stones, plus the number of prisoners captured by that player.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[nb 5]</a></sup></p>
<p>If there is disagreement about which stones are dead, then under area scoring rules, the players simply resume play to resolve the matter. The score is computed using the position after the next time the players pass consecutively. Under territory scoring, the rules are considerably more complex; however, in practice, players generally play on, and, once the status of each stone has been determined, return to the position at the time the first two consecutive passes occurred and remove the dead stones. For further information, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rules_of_Go" title="Rules of Go">Rules of Go</a>.</p>
<p>Given that the number of stones a player has on the board is directly related to the number of prisoners their opponent has taken, the resulting <i>net</i> score, <i>that is</i> the difference between Black's and White's scores, is identical under both rulesets (unless the players have passed different numbers of times during the course of the game). Thus, the net result given by the two scoring systems rarely differs by more than a point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_death">Life and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Life and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Life_and_death" title="Life and death">Life and death</a></div>
<p>While not actually mentioned in the rules of Go (at least in simpler rule sets, such as those of New Zealand and the U.S.), the concept of a <i>living</i> group of stones is necessary for a practical understanding of the game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EyesSeki_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-EyesSeki-53">[48]</a></sup></p>

<td><img alt="Go w.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Go_w.svg/20px-Go_w.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Go_w.svg/30px-Go_w.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Go_w.svg/40px-Go_w.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></td>
<td><img alt="Go b.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Go_b.svg/20px-Go_b.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Go_b.svg/30px-Go_b.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Go_b.svg/40px-Go_b.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></td>
<td><img alt="Go b.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Go_b.svg/20px-Go_b.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Go_b.svg/30px-Go_b.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Go_b.svg/40px-Go_b.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></td>

</table>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="font-size: 88%;">
<p>Examples of eyes (marked). The black groups at the top of the board are alive, as they have at least two eyes. The black groups at the bottom are dead as they only have one eye. The point marked <i>a</i> is a false eye.</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When a group of stones is mostly surrounded and has no options to connect with friendly stones elsewhere, the status of the group is either <i>alive</i>, <i>dead</i> or <i>unsettled</i>. A group of stones is said to be alive if it cannot be captured, even if the opponent is allowed to move first. Conversely, a group of stones is said to be dead if it cannot avoid capture, even if the owner of the group is allowed the first move. Otherwise, the group is said to be unsettled: the defending player can make it alive or the opponent can <i>kill</i> it, depending on who gets to play first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EyesSeki_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-EyesSeki-53">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>An "<a href="/wiki/List_of_Go_terms" title="List of Go terms">eye</a>" is an empty point or group of points surrounded by one player's stones. If the eye is surrounded by Black stones, the "suicide rule" forbids White to place a stone in a single-point eye surround by Black unless the placement results in a capture of Black stones that creates a liberty for White's new stone (or connected group). Effectively, the capture rule is applied before the suicide rule, and both are applied before White's play is completed.</p>
<p>By the interplay of the capture and suicide rules, survival for a group can be guaranteed only by having two or more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_terms#Eyes" title="Go terms">eyes</a>. If two such eyes exist, the opponent can never capture a group of stones, because one liberty is always remaining. One eye is not enough for life because a point that would normally be suicide may be filled by the opponent, thereby capturing the group. In the "Examples of eyes" diagram, all the circled points are eyes. The two black groups in the upper corners are alive, as both have at least two eyes. The groups in the lower corners are dead, as both have only one eye. The group in the lower left may seem to have two eyes, but the surrounded empty point marked <i>a</i> is not actually an eye. White can play there and take a black stone. Such a point is often called a <i>false</i> eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EyesSeki_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-EyesSeki-53">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seki_.28mutual_life.29">Seki (mutual life)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Seki (mutual life)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goseki.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="193" data-file-width="256" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Goseki.png/220px-Goseki.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/Goseki.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goseki.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Example of seki (mutual life). Neither Black nor White can play on the marked points without reducing their own liberties for those groups to one (self-atari).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is an exception to the requirement that a group must have two eyes to be alive, a situation called <i>seki</i> (or <i>mutual life</i>). Where different colored groups are adjacent and share liberties, the situation may reach a position when neither player wants to move first, because doing so would allow the opponent to capture; in such situations therefore both players' stones remain on the board in mutual life or "seki". Neither player receives any points for those groups, but at least those groups themselves remain living, as opposed to being captured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[nb 6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Seki can occur in many ways. The simplest are:</p>
<ol>
<li>each player has a group without eyes and they share two liberties, and</li>
<li>each player has a group with one eye and they share one more liberty.</li>
</ol>
<p>In the "Example of seki (mutual life)" diagram, the circled points are liberties shared by both a black and a white group. Neither player wants to play on a circled point, because doing so would allow the opponent to capture. All the other groups in this example, both black and white, are alive with at least two eyes. Seki can result from an attempt by one player to invade and kill a nearly settled group of the other player.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EyesSeki_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-EyesSeki-53">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tactics">Tactics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tactics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Go_strategy_and_tactics" title="Go strategy and tactics">Go strategy and tactics</a></div>
<p>In Go, tactics deal with immediate fighting between stones, capturing and saving stones, life, death and other issues localized to a specific part of the board. Larger issues, not limited to only part of the board, are referred to as <i>strategy</i>, and are covered in their own section.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capturing_tactics">Capturing tactics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Capturing tactics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are several tactical constructs aimed at capturing stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[49]</a></sup> These are among the first things a player learns after understanding the rules. Recognizing the possibility that stones can be captured using these techniques is an important step forward.</p>

<div class="thumbcaption" style="font-size: 88%;">
<p>A ladder. Black cannot escape unless the ladder connects to black stones further down the board that will intercept with the ladder.</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The most basic technique is the <i>ladder</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ladder_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ladder-56">[50]</a></sup> To capture stones in a ladder, a player uses a constant series of capture threats—called <i>atari</i>—to force the opponent into a zigzag pattern as shown in the adjacent diagram. Unless the pattern runs into friendly stones along the way, the stones in the ladder cannot avoid capture. Experienced players recognize the futility of continuing the pattern and play elsewhere. The presence of a ladder on the board does give a player the option to play a stone in the path of the ladder, thereby threatening to rescue their stones, forcing a response. Such a move is called a <i>ladder breaker</i> and may be a powerful strategic move. In the diagram, Black has the option of playing a ladder breaker.</p>

<div class="thumbcaption" style="font-size: 88%;">
<p>A net. The chain of three marked black stones cannot escape in any direction.</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another technique to capture stones is the so-called <i>net</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Net_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Net-57">[51]</a></sup> also known by its Japanese name, <i>geta</i>. This refers to a move that loosely surrounds some stones, preventing their escape in all directions. An example is given in the adjacent diagram. It is generally better to capture stones in a net than in a ladder, because a net does not depend on the condition that there are no opposing stones in the way, nor does it allow the opponent to play a strategic ladder breaker.</p>

<div class="thumbcaption" style="font-size: 88%;">
<p>A snapback. Although Black can capture the white stone by playing at the circled point, the resulting shape for Black has only one liberty (at 1), thus White can then capture the three black stones by playing at <b>1</b> again (<i>snap back</i>).</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A third technique to capture stones is the <i>snapback</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SnapBack_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-SnapBack-58">[52]</a></sup> In a snapback, one player allows a single stone to be captured, then immediately plays on the point formerly occupied by that stone; by so doing, the player captures a larger group of their opponent's stones, in effect <i>snapping back</i> at those stones. An example can be seen on the right. As with the ladder, an experienced player does not play out such a sequence, recognizing the futility of capturing only to be captured back immediately.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reading_ahead">Reading ahead</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reading ahead">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of the most important skills required for strong tactical play is the ability to read ahead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EGS3_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-EGS3-59">[53]</a></sup> Reading ahead includes considering available moves to play, the possible responses to each move, and the subsequent possibilities after each of those responses. Some of the strongest players of the game can read up to 40 moves ahead even in complicated positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TreasureChest_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-TreasureChest-60">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>As explained in the scoring rules, some stone formations can never be captured and are said to be alive, while other stones may be in the position where they cannot avoid being captured and are said to be dead. Much of the practice material available to players of the game comes in the form of life and death problems, also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Tsumego" title="Tsumego">tsumego</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsumego_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsumego-61">[55]</a></sup> In such problems, players are challenged to find the vital move sequence that kills a group of the opponent or saves a group of their own. Tsumego are considered an excellent way to train a player's ability at reading ahead,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsumego_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tsumego-61">[55]</a></sup> and are available for all skill levels, some posing a challenge even to top players.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ko_fighting">Ko fighting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ko fighting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gokof.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="561" data-file-width="560" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Gokof.png/220px-Gokof.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Gokof.png/330px-Gokof.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Gokof.png/440px-Gokof.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gokof.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A simplified ko fight on a 9×9 board. The ko is at the point marked with a square—Black has "taken the ko" first. The ko fight determines the life of the A and B groups—only one survives and the other is captured. White may play C as a ko threat, and Black properly answers at D. White can then "take the ko" by playing at the square-marked point (capturing the one black stone). E is a possible ko threat for Black.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In situations when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ko_rule" title="Ko rule">Ko rule</a> applies, a ko fight may occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ko_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ko-42">[38]</a></sup> If the player who is prohibited from capture is of the opinion that the capture is important, because it prevents a large group of stones from being captured for instance, the player may play a <i>ko threat</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ko_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ko-42">[38]</a></sup> This is a move elsewhere on the board that threatens to make a large profit if the opponent does not respond. If the opponent does respond to the ko threat, the situation on the board has changed, and the prohibition on capturing the ko no longer applies. Thus the player who made the ko threat may now recapture the ko. Their opponent is then in the same situation and can either play a ko threat as well, or concede the ko by simply playing elsewhere. If a player concedes the ko, either because they do not think it important or because there are no moves left that could function as a ko threat, they have <i>lost</i> the ko, and their opponent may connect the ko.</p>
<p>Instead of responding to a ko threat, a player may also choose to <i>ignore</i> the threat and connect the ko.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ko_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ko-42">[38]</a></sup> They thereby win the ko, but at a cost. The choice of when to respond to a threat and when to ignore it is a subtle one, which requires a player to consider many factors, including how much is gained by connecting, how much is lost by not responding, how many possible ko threats both players have remaining, what the optimal order of playing them is, and what the <i>size</i>—points lost or gained—of each of the remaining threats is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tavernier_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tavernier-62">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frequently, the winner of the ko fight does not connect the ko but instead captures one of the chains that constituted their opponent's side of the ko.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ko_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ko-42">[38]</a></sup> In some cases, this leads to another ko fight at a neighboring location.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Strategy">Strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. </tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Go_strategy_and_tactics" title="Go strategy and tactics">Go strategy and tactics</a></div>
<p>Strategy deals with global influence, interaction between distant stones, keeping the whole board in mind during local fights, and other issues that involve the overall game. It is therefore possible to allow a tactical loss when it confers a strategic advantage.</p>
<p>Novices often start by randomly placing stones on the board, as if it were a game of chance. An understanding of how stones connect for greater power develops, and then a few basic <a href="/wiki/Go_opening_theory" title="Go opening theory">common opening sequences</a> may be understood. Learning the ways of life and death helps in a fundamental way to develop one's strategic understanding of <i>weak groups</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LifeDeath_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-LifeDeath-63">[nb 7]</a></sup> A player who both plays aggressively and can handle adversity is said to display <i><a href="/wiki/Kiai" title="Kiai">kiai</a></i>, or fighting spirit, in the game.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_concepts">Basic concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Basic concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(February 2016)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_terms" title="Go terms">Go terms</a></div>
<p>Basic strategic aspects include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Connection: Keeping one's own stones connected means that fewer groups need to make living shape, and one has fewer groups to defend.</li>
<li>Cut: Keeping opposing stones disconnected means that the opponent needs to defend and make living shape for more groups.</li>
<li>Stay alive: The simplest way to stay alive is to establish a foothold in the corner or along one of the sides. At a minimum, a group must have two eyes (separate open points) to be "alive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[57]</a></sup> An opponent cannot fill in either eye, as any such move is suicidal and prohibited in the rules.</li>
<li>Mutual life (seki) is better than dying: A situation in which neither player can play on a particular point without then allowing the other player to play at another point to capture. The most common example is that of adjacent groups that share their last few liberties—if either player plays in the shared liberties, they can reduce their own group to a single liberty (putting themselves in <i>atari</i>), allowing their opponent to capture it on the next move.</li>
<li>Death: A group that lacks living shape is eventually removed from the board as captured.</li>
<li>Invasion: Set up a new living group inside an area where the opponent has greater influence, means one reduces the opponent's score in proportion to the area one occupies.</li>
<li>Reduction: Placing a stone far enough into the opponent's area of influence to reduce the amount of territory they eventually get, but not so far in that it can be cut off from friendly stones outside.</li>
<li>Sente: A play that forces one's opponent to respond (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_terms" title="Go terms">gote</a></i>). A player who can regularly play <i>sente</i> has the initiative and can control the flow of the game.</li>
<li>Sacrifice: Allowing a group to die in order to carry out a play, or plan, in a more important area.</li>
</ul>
<p>The strategy involved can become very abstract and complex. High-level players spend years improving their understanding of strategy, and a novice may play many hundreds of games against opponents before being able to win regularly.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opening_strategy">Opening strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Opening strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the opening of the game, players usually play in the corners of the board first, as the presence of two edges makes it easier for them to surround territory and establish their stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Opening_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Opening-65">[58]</a></sup> After the corners, focus moves to the sides, where there is still one edge to support a player's stones. Opening moves are generally on the third and fourth line from the edge, with occasional moves on the second and fifth lines. In general, stones on the third line offer stability and are good defensive moves, whereas stones on the fourth line influence more of the board and are good attacking moves. The opening is the most difficult part of the game for professional players and takes a disproportionate amount of the playing time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITB_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITB-66">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the opening, players often play established sequences called <a href="/wiki/Joseki" title="Joseki">joseki</a>, which are locally balanced exchanges;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joseki_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joseki-67">[60]</a></sup> however, the joseki chosen should also produce a satisfactory result on a global scale. It is generally advisable to keep a balance between territory and influence. Which of these gets precedence is often a matter of individual taste.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_phase_and_endgame">Middle phase and endgame</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Middle phase and endgame">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The middle phase of the game is the most combative, and usually lasts for more than 100 moves. During the <i>middlegame</i>, the players invade each other's territories, and attack formations that lack the necessary <i>two eyes</i> for viability. Such groups may be saved or sacrificed for something more significant on the board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Think_Big_in_Go_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Think_Big_in_Go-68">[61]</a></sup> It is possible that one player may succeed in capturing a large weak group of the opponent's, which often proves decisive and ends the game by a resignation. However, matters may be more complex yet, with major trade-offs, apparently dead groups reviving, and skillful play to attack in such a way as to construct territories rather than kill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Induction_in_Go_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Induction_in_Go-69">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The end of the middlegame and transition to the endgame is marked by a few features. The game breaks up into areas that do not affect each other (with a caveat about <i>ko</i> fights), where before the central area of the board related to all parts of it. No large weak groups are still in serious danger. Moves can reasonably be attributed some definite value, such as 20 points or fewer, rather than simply being necessary to compete. Both players set limited objectives in their plans, in making or destroying territory, capturing or saving stones. These changing aspects of the game usually occur at much the same time, for strong players. In brief, the middlegame switches into the endgame when the concepts of strategy and influence need reassessment in terms of concrete final results on the board.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_in_China">Origin in China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Origin in China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:19x19_Sui_Dynasty_Go_Board.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2018" data-file-width="2572" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/19x19_Sui_Dynasty_Go_Board.JPG/220px-19x19_Sui_Dynasty_Go_Board.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/19x19_Sui_Dynasty_Go_Board.JPG/330px-19x19_Sui_Dynasty_Go_Board.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/19x19_Sui_Dynasty_Go_Board.JPG/440px-19x19_Sui_Dynasty_Go_Board.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:19x19_Sui_Dynasty_Go_Board.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
19x19 Go Board from Sui Dynasty</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The earliest written reference to the game is generally recognized as the historical annal <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zuo_Zhuan" title="Zuo Zhuan">Zuo Zhuan</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Tso_Chuan_book_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Tso_Chuan_book-5">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[3]</a></sup> (c. 4th century BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chronology2_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-chronology2-7">[4]</a></sup> referring to a historical event of 548 BC. It is also mentioned in Book XVII of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analects_of_Confucius" title="Analects of Confucius">Analects of Confucius</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chronology2_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-chronology2-7">[4]</a></sup> and in two books written by <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Confucius_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Confucius-70">[63]</a></sup> (c. 3rd century BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chronology2_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-chronology2-7">[4]</a></sup> In all of these works, the game is referred to as <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ISO 7098 Chinese"><i>yì</i></span> (<span lang="zh-Hant" xml:lang="zh-Hant">弈</span>). Today, in China, it is known as <i><b>weiqi</b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans" xml:lang="zh-Hans">围棋</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant" xml:lang="zh-Hant">圍棋</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin"><span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Zh-w%C3%A9iq%C3%AD.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0f/Zh-w%C3%A9iq%C3%AD.ogg" title="Zh-wéiqí.ogg">wéiqí</a></span></span></span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile" xml:lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">wei ch'i</span></i>), literally "encirclement board game".</p>
<p>Go was originally played on a 17×17 line grid, but a 19×19 grid became standard by the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> (618–907).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[3]</a></sup> Legends trace the origin of the game to the mythical <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Chinese emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Yao" title="Emperor Yao">Yao</a> (2337–2258 BC), who was said to have had his counselor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shun_(Chinese_leader)" title="Shun (Chinese leader)">Shun</a> design it for his unruly son, <a href="/wiki/Danzhu" title="Danzhu">Danzhu</a>, to favorably influence him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[64]</a></sup> Other theories suggest that the game was derived from Chinese tribal warlords and generals, who used pieces of stone to map out attacking positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origin_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origin-72">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In China, Go was considered one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Arts_of_the_Chinese_Scholar" title="Four Arts of the Chinese Scholar">four cultivated arts</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Junzi" title="Junzi">Chinese scholar gentleman</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">calligraphy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">painting</a> and playing the musical instrument <a href="/wiki/Guqin" title="Guqin">guqin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FourAccomp_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FourAccomp-74">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread_to_Korea_and_Japan">Spread to Korea and Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Spread to Korea and Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Weiqi was introduced to Korea sometime between the 5th and 7th centuries CE, and was popular among the higher classes. In Korea, the game is called <i><b>baduk</b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">hangul</a>: <span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">바둑</span>), and a variant of the game called <a href="/wiki/Sunjang_baduk" title="Sunjang baduk">Sunjang baduk</a> was developed by the 16th century. Sunjang baduk became the main variant played in Korea until the end of the 19th century, when the current version was reintroduced from Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-histKorea_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-histKorea-76">[69]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korean_Game_from_the_Carpenter_Collection,_ca._1910-1920.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1023" data-file-width="1490" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Korean_Game_from_the_Carpenter_Collection%2C_ca._1910-1920.jpg/220px-Korean_Game_from_the_Carpenter_Collection%2C_ca._1910-1920.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Korean_Game_from_the_Carpenter_Collection%2C_ca._1910-1920.jpg/330px-Korean_Game_from_the_Carpenter_Collection%2C_ca._1910-1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Korean_Game_from_the_Carpenter_Collection%2C_ca._1910-1920.jpg/440px-Korean_Game_from_the_Carpenter_Collection%2C_ca._1910-1920.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Korean_Game_from_the_Carpenter_Collection,_ca._1910-1920.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Korean couple, in traditional dress, play in a photograph dated between 1910 and 1920.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:137px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GO_Tale_Genji_Takekawa.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1000" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/GO_Tale_Genji_Takekawa.JPG/135px-GO_Tale_Genji_Takekawa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/GO_Tale_Genji_Takekawa.JPG/203px-GO_Tale_Genji_Takekawa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/GO_Tale_Genji_Takekawa.JPG/270px-GO_Tale_Genji_Takekawa.JPG 2x" width="135"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GO_Tale_Genji_Takekawa.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustrated <a href="/wiki/Handscroll" title="Handscroll">handscroll</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i> (12th century).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The game reached Japan in the 7th century CE—where it is called <i><b>go</b></i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">碁</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span> or <i><b>igo</b></i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">囲碁</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span>—the game became popular at the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Court_in_Kyoto" title="Imperial Court in Kyoto">Japanese imperial court</a> in the 8th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistJapan2_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-HistJapan2-77">[70]</a></sup> and among the general public by the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistJapan3_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-HistJapan3-78">[71]</a></sup> The modern version of the game as we know it today was formalized in Japan in the 15th century CE. In 1603, <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Ieyasu" title="Tokugawa Ieyasu">Tokugawa Ieyasu</a> re-established Japan's unified national government. In the same year, he assigned the then-best player in Japan, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> monk named Nikkai (né Kanō Yosaburo, 1559), to the post of <a href="/wiki/Godokoro" title="Godokoro">Godokoro</a> (Minister of Go).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-79">[72]</a></sup> Nikkai took the name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honinbo_Sansa" title="Honinbo Sansa">Honinbo Sansa</a> and founded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honinbo_school" title="Honinbo school">Honinbo Go school</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-79">[72]</a></sup> Several <a href="/wiki/Four_go_houses" title="Four go houses">competing schools</a> were founded soon after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-79">[72]</a></sup> These officially recognized and subsidized Go schools greatly developed the level of play and introduced the <a href="/wiki/Go_ranks_and_ratings" title="Go ranks and ratings">dan/kyu style system</a> of ranking players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ranks_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-ranks-80">[73]</a></sup> Players from the four schools (Honinbo, Yasui, Inoue and Hayashi) competed in the annual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castle_game" title="Castle game">castle games</a>, played in the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Shogun" title="Shogun">shogun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-castlegames_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-castlegames-81">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internationalization">Internationalization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Internationalization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Despite its widespread popularity in East Asia, Go has been slow to spread to the rest of the world. Although there are some mentions of the game in western literature from the 16th century forward, Go did not start to become popular in the West until the end of the 19th century, when German scientist <a href="/wiki/Oskar_Korschelt" title="Oskar Korschelt">Oskar Korschelt</a> wrote a treatise on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Science_and_Technology_in_China" title="History of Science and Technology in China">ancient Han Chinese</a> game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoHistEU_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoHistEU-82">[75]</a></sup> By the early 20th century, Go had spread throughout the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austro-Hungarian</a> empires. In 1905, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lasker" title="Edward Lasker">Edward Lasker</a> learned the game while in Berlin. When he moved to New York, Lasker founded the New York Go Club together with (amongst others) Arthur Smith, who had learned of the game in Japan while touring the East and had published the book <i>The Game of Go</i> in 1908.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agahbk95_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-agahbk95-83">[76]</a></sup> Lasker's book <i>Go and Go-moku</i> (1934) helped spread the game throughout the U.S.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agahbk95_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-agahbk95-83">[76]</a></sup> and in 1935, the American Go Association was formed. Two years later, in 1937, the German Go Association was founded.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> put a stop to most Go activity, since it was a game coming from Japan, but after the war, Go continued to spread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[77]</a></sup> For most of the 20th century, the <a href="/wiki/Nihon_Ki-in" title="Nihon Ki-in">Japan Go Association (Nihon Ki-in)</a> played a leading role in spreading Go outside East Asia by publishing the English-language magazine <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Go_Review&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Go Review (page does not exist)">Go Review</a></i> in the 1960s, establishing <a href="/wiki/Go_centers" title="Go centers">Go centers</a> in the U.S., Europe and South America, and often sending professional teachers on tour to Western nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ProVisits_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-ProVisits-85">[78]</a></sup> Internationally, the game had been commonly known since the start of the twentieth century by its shortened Japanese name, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_terms" title="Go terms">terms for common Go concepts</a> are derived from their Japanese pronunciation.</p>
<p>In 1996, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> astronaut <a href="/wiki/Daniel_T._Barry" title="Daniel T. Barry">Daniel Barry</a> and Japanese astronaut <a href="/wiki/Koichi_Wakata" title="Koichi Wakata">Koichi Wakata</a> became the first people to play Go in space. They used a special Go set, which was named Go Space, designed by Wai-Cheung Willson Chow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SvenskGo_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-SvenskGo-86">[79]</a></sup> Both astronauts were awarded honorary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dan_rank" title="Dan rank">dan ranks</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Nihon_Ki-in" title="Nihon Ki-in">Nihon Ki-in</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SvenskGo_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-SvenskGo-86">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of December 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Go_Federation" title="International Go Federation">International Go Federation</a> has 75 member countries, with 67 member countries outside <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-igf-members_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-igf-members-15">[12]</a></sup></p>
