<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competitive_play">Competitive play</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Competitive play">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ranks_and_ratings">Ranks and ratings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Ranks and ratings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Go_ranks_and_ratings" title="Go ranks and ratings">Go ranks and ratings</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Go_pros_and_amateurs.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Go_pros_and_amateurs.jpeg/220px-Go_pros_and_amateurs.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Go_pros_and_amateurs.jpeg/330px-Go_pros_and_amateurs.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Go_pros_and_amateurs.jpeg/440px-Go_pros_and_amateurs.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Go_pros_and_amateurs.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Three Japanese professional Go players observe some younger amateurs as they dissect a <a href="/wiki/Life_and_death" title="Life and death">life and death</a> problem in the corner of the board, at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Go_Congress" title="US Go Congress">US Go Congress</a> in <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>, Texas, 2003.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Go, rank indicates a player's skill in the game. Traditionally, ranks are measured using <i>kyu</i> and <i>dan</i> grades,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[80]</a></sup> a system also adopted by many <a href="/wiki/Martial_arts" title="Martial arts">martial arts</a>. More recently, mathematical rating systems similar to the <a href="/wiki/Elo_rating_system" title="Elo rating system">Elo rating system</a> have been introduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EGFRating_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-EGFRating-88">[81]</a></sup> Such rating systems often provide a mechanism for converting a rating to a kyu or dan grade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EGFRating_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-EGFRating-88">[81]</a></sup> Kyu grades (abbreviated <i>k</i>) are considered student grades and decrease as playing level increases, meaning 1st kyu is the strongest available kyu grade. Dan grades (abbreviated <i>d</i>) are considered master grades, and increase from 1st dan to 7th dan. <a href="/wiki/Shodan_(rank)" title="Shodan (rank)">First dan</a> equals a <a href="/wiki/Black_belt_(martial_arts)" title="Black belt (martial arts)">black belt</a> in eastern martial arts using this system. The difference among each amateur rank is one handicap stone. For example, if a 5k plays a game with a 1k, the 5k would need a handicap of four stones to even the odds. Top-level amateur players sometimes defeat professionals in tournament play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EGFDatabase_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-EGFDatabase-89">[82]</a></sup> Professional players have <i><a href="/wiki/Go_professional" title="Go professional">professional dan ranks</a></i> (abbreviated <i>p</i>). These ranks are separate from amateur ranks.</p>
<p>The rank system comprises, from the lowest to highest ranks:</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th>Rank Type</th>
<th>Range</th>
<th>Stage</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Double-digit <i>kyu</i></td>
<td>30–21k</td>
<td>Beginner</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Double-digit <i>kyu</i></td>
<td>20–10k</td>
<td>Casual player</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Single-digit <i>kyu</i></td>
<td>9–1k</td>
<td>Intermediate/club player</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Amateur <i>dan</i></td>
<td>1–7d (where 8d is a special title)</td>
<td>Advanced player</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Professional <i>dan</i></td>
<td>1–9p (where 10p is a special title)</td>
<td>Professionals</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tournament_and_match_rules">Tournament and match rules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Tournament and match rules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_competitions" title="Go competitions">Go competitions</a></div>
<p>Tournament and match rules deal with factors that may influence the game but are not part of the actual rules of play. Such rules may differ between events. Rules that influence the game include: the setting of compensation points (<i><a href="/wiki/Komidashi" title="Komidashi">komi</a></i>), handicap, and time control parameters. Rules that do not generally influence the game are: the tournament system, pairing strategies, and placement criteria.</p>
<p>Common tournament systems used in Go include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss_system_tournament#McMahon_system" title="Swiss system tournament">McMahon system</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[83]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss_system_tournament" title="Swiss system tournament">Swiss system</a>, <a href="/wiki/League_system" title="League system">league systems</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Single-elimination_tournament" title="Single-elimination tournament">knockout system</a>. Tournaments may combine multiple systems; many <a href="/wiki/List_of_professional_Go_tournaments" title="List of professional Go tournaments">professional Go tournaments</a> use a combination of the league and knockout systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tournament rules may also set the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>compensation points, called komi, which compensate the second player for the first move advantage of his opponent; tournaments commonly use a compensation in the range of 5–8 points,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-histKomi_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-histKomi-92">[85]</a></sup> generally including a half-point to prevent draws;</li>
<li>handicap stones placed on the board before alternate play, allowing players of different strengths to play competitively (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_handicap" title="Go handicap">Go handicap</a> for more information); and</li>
<li><i>superko</i>: Although the basic ko rule described above covers more than 95% of all cycles occurring in games,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KoRules_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-KoRules-93">[86]</a></sup> there are some complex situations—<i><a href="/wiki/Ko_fight#Complex_situations_involving_ko" title="Ko fight">triple ko</a></i>, <i>eternal life</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[nb 8]</a></sup> etc.—that are not covered by it but would allow the game to cycle indefinitely. To prevent this, the ko rule is sometimes extended to disallow the repetition of <i>any</i> previous position. This extension is called superko.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KoRules_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-KoRules-93">[86]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Top_players_and_professional_go">Top players and professional go</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Top players and professional go">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_players" title="Go players">Go players</a>, <a href="/wiki/Go_professional" title="Go professional">Go professional</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_professional_Go_tournaments" title="List of professional Go tournaments">List of professional Go tournaments</a></div>
<p>A Go professional is a professional player of the game of Go. There are six areas with professional go associations, these are: China (<a href="/wiki/China_Qiyuan" title="China Qiyuan">China Qiyuan</a>), Japan (<a href="/wiki/Nihon_Ki-in" title="Nihon Ki-in">Nihon Ki-in</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansai_Ki-in" title="Kansai Ki-in">Kansai Ki-in</a>), South Korea (<a href="/wiki/Korea_Baduk_Association" title="Korea Baduk Association">Korea Baduk Association</a>), Taiwan (<a href="/wiki/Taiwan_Chi_Yuan_Culture_Foundation" title="Taiwan Chi Yuan Culture Foundation">Taiwan Chi Yuan Culture Foundation</a>), the United States (<a href="/wiki/American_Go_Association#AGA_Professional_Players" title="American Go Association">AGA Professional System</a>) and Europe (<a href="/wiki/European_Go_Federation" title="European Go Federation">European Professional System</a>).</p>
<p>Although the game was developed in China, the establishment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Go_houses" title="Four Go houses">Four Go houses</a> by <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Ieyasu" title="Tokugawa Ieyasu">Tokugawa Ieyasu</a> at the start of the 17th century shifted the focus of the Go world to Japan. State sponsorship, allowing players to dedicate themselves full-time to study of the game, and fierce competition between individual houses resulted in a significant increase in the level of play. During this period, the best player of his generation was given the prestigious title <a href="/wiki/Meijin" title="Meijin">Meijin</a> (master) and the post of <a href="/wiki/Godokoro" title="Godokoro">Godokoro</a> (minister of Go). Of special note are the players who were dubbed <a href="/wiki/Kisei" title="Kisei">Kisei</a> (Go Sage). The only three players to receive this honor were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dosaku" title="Dosaku">Dosaku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hon%27inb%C5%8D_J%C5%8Dwa" title="Hon'inbō Jōwa">Jowa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honinbo_Shusaku" title="Honinbo Shusaku">Shusaku</a>, all of the house <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honinbo" title="Honinbo">Honinbo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msoworld.com_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-msoworld.com-95">[87]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GovsShusai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="150" data-file-width="230" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/GovsShusai.jpg/220px-GovsShusai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/GovsShusai.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GovsShusai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honinbo_Shusai" title="Honinbo Shusai">Honinbo Shusai</a> (left), last head of house Honinbo, plays against then-up-and-coming <a href="/wiki/Go_Seigen" title="Go Seigen">Go Seigen</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_game_of_the_century_(Go)" title="The game of the century (Go)">game of the century</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the end of the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> period, the Go houses slowly disappeared, and in 1924, the <a href="/wiki/Nihon_Ki-in" title="Nihon Ki-in">Nihon Ki-in</a> (Japanese Go Association) was formed. Top players from this period often played newspaper-sponsored matches of 2–10 games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newspaper_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-newspaper-96">[88]</a></sup> Of special note are the (Chinese-born) player <a href="/wiki/Go_Seigen" title="Go Seigen">Go Seigen</a> (Chinese: Wu Qingyuan), who scored 80% in these matches and beat down most of his opponents to inferior handicaps),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[89]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Minoru_Kitani" title="Minoru Kitani">Minoru Kitani</a>, who dominated matches in the early 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[90]</a></sup> These two players are also recognized for their groundbreaking work on new <a href="/wiki/Go_opening_theory" title="Go opening theory">opening theory</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shinfuseki" title="Shinfuseki">Shinfuseki</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShinFuseki_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShinFuseki-99">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>For much of the 20th century, Go continued to be dominated by players trained in Japan. Notable names included <a href="/wiki/Eio_Sakata" title="Eio Sakata">Eio Sakata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rin_Kaiho" title="Rin Kaiho">Rin Kaiho</a> (born in China), <a href="/wiki/Masao_Kato" title="Masao Kato">Masao Kato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koichi_Kobayashi" title="Koichi Kobayashi">Koichi Kobayashi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cho_Chikun" title="Cho Chikun">Cho Chikun</a> (born Cho Ch'i-hun, from South Korea).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[92]</a></sup> Top Chinese and Korean talents often moved to Japan, because the level of play there was high and funding was more lavish. One of the first Korean players to do so was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cho_Namchul" title="Cho Namchul">Cho Namchul</a>, who studied in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kitani_Dojo" title="Kitani Dojo">Kitani Dojo</a> 1937–1944. After his return to Korea, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanguk_Kiwon" title="Hanguk Kiwon">Hanguk Kiwon</a> (Korea Baduk Association) was formed and caused the level of play in South Korea to rise significantly in the second half of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[93]</a></sup> In China, the game declined during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a> (1966–1976) but quickly recovered in the last quarter of the 20th century, bringing Chinese players, such as <a href="/wiki/Nie_Weiping" title="Nie Weiping">Nie Weiping</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ma_Xiaochun" title="Ma Xiaochun">Ma Xiaochun</a>, on par with their Japanese and South Korean counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[94]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Weiqi_Association" title="Chinese Weiqi Association">Chinese Weiqi Association</a> (today part of the China Qiyuan) was established in 1962, professional dan grades started being issued in 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[95]</a></sup> Western professional go began in 2012 with the American Go Association's Professional System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[96]</a></sup> In 2014, the European Go Federation followed suit and started their professional system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[97]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Changho-Dinerchtein.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="357" data-file-width="500" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Changho-Dinerchtein.jpg/220px-Changho-Dinerchtein.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Changho-Dinerchtein.jpg/330px-Changho-Dinerchtein.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Changho-Dinerchtein.jpg/440px-Changho-Dinerchtein.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Changho-Dinerchtein.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
South Korean player <a href="/wiki/Lee_Chang-ho" title="Lee Chang-ho">Lee Chang-ho</a> plays against Russian player <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Dinerchtein" title="Alexandre Dinerchtein">Alexandre Dinerchtein</a>, seven-time European Champion and one of the few non-East Asian players to reach <a href="/wiki/Go_professional" title="Go professional">professional</a> status.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the advent of major international titles from 1989 onward, it became possible to compare the level of players from different countries more accurately. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cho_Hunhyun" title="Cho Hunhyun">Cho Hunhyun</a> of South Korea won the first edition of the Quadrennial <a href="/wiki/Ing_Cup" title="Ing Cup">Ing Cup</a> in 1989. His disciple <a href="/wiki/Lee_Chang-ho" title="Lee Chang-ho">Lee Chang-ho</a> was the dominant player in international Go competitions for more than a decade spanning much of 1990s and early 2000s; he is also credited with groundbreaking works on the endgame. Cho, Lee and other South Korean players such as <a href="/wiki/Seo_Bong-soo" title="Seo Bong-soo">Seo Bong-soo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yoo_Changhyuk" title="Yoo Changhyuk">Yoo Changhyuk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lee_Sedol" title="Lee Sedol">Lee Sedol</a> between them won majority of international titles in this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[98]</a></sup> Several Chinese players also rose to the top in international Go from 2000s, most notably <a href="/wiki/Ma_Xiaochun" title="Ma Xiaochun">Ma Xiaochun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chang_Hao" title="Chang Hao">Chang Hao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gu_Li" title="Gu Li">Gu Li</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ke_Jie" title="Ke Jie">Ke Jie</a>. As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Japan lags behind in the international Go scene.</p>
<p>Historically, as with most sports and games, more men than women have played Go. Special tournaments for women exist, but until recently, men and women did not compete together at the highest levels; however, the creation of new, open tournaments and the rise of strong female players, most notably <a href="/wiki/Rui_Naiwei" title="Rui Naiwei">Rui Naiwei</a>, have in recent years highlighted the strength and competitiveness of emerging female players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shotwell2003_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-shotwell2003-107">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>The level in other countries has traditionally been much lower, except for some players who had preparatory professional training in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[nb 9]</a></sup> Knowledge of the game has been scant elsewhere up until the 20th century. A famous player of the 1920s was <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lasker" title="Edward Lasker">Edward Lasker</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[nb 10]</a></sup> It was not until the 1950s that more than a few Western players took up the game as other than a passing interest. In 1978, <a href="/wiki/Manfred_Wimmer" title="Manfred Wimmer">Manfred Wimmer</a> became the first Westerner to receive a professional player's certificate from an East Asian professional Go association.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wimmer_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-wimmer-110">[100]</a></sup> In 2000, American <a href="/wiki/Michael_Redmond_(Go_player)" title="Michael Redmond (Go player)">Michael Redmond</a> became the first Western player to achieve a 9 dan rank.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment">Equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Go_equipment" title="Go equipment">Go equipment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Go-tisch.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="250" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Go-tisch.png/220px-Go-tisch.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d0/Go-tisch.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Go-tisch.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A traditional Japanese set, with a solid wooden floor board (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">碁盤</span></span> <i>goban</i>), 2 bowls (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">碁笥</span></span> <i>goke</i>) and 361 stones (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">碁石</span></span> <i>goishi</i>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It is possible to play Go with a simple paper board and coins or plastic tokens for the stones. More popular midrange equipment includes cardstock, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laminate" title="Laminate">laminated</a> <a href="/wiki/Particle_board" title="Particle board">particle board</a>, or wood boards with stones of plastic or glass. More expensive traditional materials are still used by many players. The most expensive Go sets have black stones carved from slate and white stones carved from translucent white shells, played on boards carved in a single piece from the trunk of a tree.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_equipment">Traditional equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Traditional equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Boards">Boards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Boards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_board" title="Go board">Go board</a> (generally referred to by its Japanese name <i>goban</i> <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">碁盤</span></span>) typically measures between 45 and 48 cm (18 and 19 in) in length (from one player's side to the other) and 42 to 44 cm (17 to 17 in) in width. Chinese boards are slightly larger, as a traditional Chinese Go stone is slightly larger to match. The board is not square; there is a 15:14 ratio in length to width, because with a perfectly square board, from the player's viewing angle the perspective creates a foreshortening of the board. The added length compensates for this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipSize_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-EquipSize-111">[101]</a></sup> There are two main types of boards: a table board similar in most respects to other gameboards like that used for chess, and a floor board, which is its own free-standing table and at which the players sit.</p>
<p>The traditional Japanese <i>goban</i> is between 10 and 18 cm (3.9 and 7.1 in) thick and has legs; it sits on the floor (see picture).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipSize_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-EquipSize-111">[101]</a></sup> It is preferably made from the rare golden-tinged <a href="/wiki/Torreya_nucifera" title="Torreya nucifera">Kaya</a> tree (<i>Torreya nucifera</i>), with the very best made from Kaya trees up to 700 years old. More recently, the related <a href="/wiki/Torreya_californica" title="Torreya californica">California Torreya</a> (<i>Torreya californica</i>) has been prized for its light color and pale rings as well as its reduced expense and more readily available stock. The natural resources of Japan have been unable to keep up with the enormous demand for the slow-growing Kaya trees; both <i>T. nucifera</i> and <i>T. californica</i> take many hundreds of years to grow to the necessary size, and they are now extremely rare, raising the price of such equipment tremendously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipWood_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-EquipWood-112">[102]</a></sup> As Kaya trees are a protected species in Japan, they cannot be harvested until they have died. Thus, an old-growth, floor-standing Kaya <i>goban</i> can easily cost in excess of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USD" title="USD">$</a>10,000 with the highest-quality examples costing more than $60,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kayaprice_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-kayaprice-113">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other, less expensive woods often used to make quality table boards in both Chinese and Japanese dimensions include <a href="/wiki/Thujopsis" title="Thujopsis">Hiba</a> (<i>Thujopsis dolabrata</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Katsura_tree" title="Katsura tree">Katsura</a> (<i>Cercidiphyllum japonicum</i>), <a href="/wiki/Agathis" title="Agathis">Kauri</a> (<i>Agathis</i>), and Shin Kaya (various varieties of <a href="/wiki/Spruce" title="Spruce">spruce</a>, commonly from Alaska, Siberia and China's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yunnan_Province" title="Yunnan Province">Yunnan Province</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipWood_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-EquipWood-112">[102]</a></sup> So-called <i>Shin Kaya</i> is a potentially confusing merchant's term: <i>shin</i> means "new", and thus <i>shin kaya</i> is best translated "faux kaya", because the woods so described are biologically unrelated to Kaya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipWood_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-EquipWood-112">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stones">Stones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Stones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A full set of Go stones (<i>goishi</i>) usually contains 181 black stones and 180 white ones; a 19×19 grid has 361 points, so there are enough stones to cover the board, and Black gets the extra odd stone because that player goes first.</p>
<p>Traditional Japanese stones are double-convex, and made of <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clamshell</a> (white) and <a href="/wiki/Slate" title="Slate">slate</a> (black).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipStones_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-EquipStones-114">[104]</a></sup> The classic slate is nachiguro stone mined in <a href="/wiki/Wakayama_Prefecture" title="Wakayama Prefecture">Wakayama Prefecture</a> and the clamshell from the Hamaguri clam; however, due to a scarcity in the Japanese supply of this clam, the stones are most often made of shells harvested from <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipStones_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-EquipStones-114">[104]</a></sup> Historically, the most prized stones were made of <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a>, often given to the reigning emperor as a gift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipStones_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-EquipStones-114">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>In China, the game is traditionally played with single-convex stones<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipStones_114-3"><a href="#cite_note-EquipStones-114">[104]</a></sup> made of a composite called <a href="/wiki/Yunzi" title="Yunzi">Yunzi</a>. The material comes from Yunnan Province and is made by <a href="/wiki/Sintering" title="Sintering">sintering</a> a proprietary and trade-secret mixture of mineral compounds derived from the local stone. This process dates to the Tang Dynasty and, after the knowledge was lost in the 1920s during the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a>, was rediscovered in the 1960s by the now state-run Yunzi company. The material is praised for its colors, its pleasing sound as compared to glass or to synthetics such as <a href="/wiki/Melamine" title="Melamine">melamine</a>, and its lower cost as opposed to other materials such as slate/shell. The term "yunzi" can also refer to a single-convex stone made of any material; however, most English-language Go suppliers specify Yunzi as a material and single-convex as a shape to avoid confusion, as stones made of Yunzi are also available in double-convex while synthetic stones can be either shape.</p>
<p>Traditional stones are made so that black stones are slightly larger in diameter than white; this is to compensate for the optical illusion created by contrasting colors that would make equal-sized white stones appear larger on the board than black stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipStones_114-4"><a href="#cite_note-EquipStones-114">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[nb 11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bowls_dwindsor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="502" data-file-width="750" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Bowls_dwindsor.jpg/220px-Bowls_dwindsor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Bowls_dwindsor.jpg/330px-Bowls_dwindsor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Bowls_dwindsor.jpg/440px-Bowls_dwindsor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bowls_dwindsor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An example of single-convex stones and <i>Go Seigen</i> bowls. These particular stones are made of <a href="/wiki/Yunzi" title="Yunzi">Yunzi</a> material, and the bowls of jujube wood.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bowls">Bowls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Bowls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The bowls for the stones are shaped like a flattened sphere with a level underside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquipBowls_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-EquipBowls-116">[105]</a></sup> The lid is loose fitting and upturned before play to receive stones captured during the game. Chinese bowls are slightly larger, and a little more rounded, a style known generally as <i>Go Seigen</i>; Japanese <i>Kitani</i> bowls tend to have a shape closer to that of the bowl of a <a href="/wiki/Snifter" title="Snifter">snifter</a> glass, such as for <a href="/wiki/Brandy" title="Brandy">brandy</a>. The bowls are usually made of turned wood. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mulberry" title="Mulberry">Mulberry</a> is the traditional material for Japanese bowls, but is very expensive; wood from the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Jujube" title="Jujube">jujube</a> date tree, which has a lighter color (it is often stained) and slightly more visible grain pattern, is a common substitute for rosewood, and traditional for Go Seigen-style bowls. Other traditional materials used for making Chinese bowls include <a href="/wiki/Lacquer" title="Lacquer">lacquered</a> wood, <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramics</a>, stone and woven straw or <a href="/wiki/Rattan" title="Rattan">rattan</a>. The names of the bowl shapes, "Go Seigen" and "Kitani", were introduced in the last quarter of the 20th century by the professional player <a href="/wiki/Janice_Kim" title="Janice Kim">Janice Kim</a> as homage to two 20th-century professional Go players by the same names, of Chinese and Japanese nationality, respectively, who are referred to as the "Fathers of modern Go".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msoworld.com_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-msoworld.com-95">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Playing_technique_and_etiquette">Playing technique and etiquette</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Playing technique and etiquette">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Playing_weiqi_in_Shanghai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Playing_weiqi_in_Shanghai.jpg/220px-Playing_weiqi_in_Shanghai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Playing_weiqi_in_Shanghai.jpg/330px-Playing_weiqi_in_Shanghai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Playing_weiqi_in_Shanghai.jpg/440px-Playing_weiqi_in_Shanghai.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Playing_weiqi_in_Shanghai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Go players in Shanghai demonstrate the traditional technique of holding a stone.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The traditional way to place a Go stone is to first take one from the bowl, gripping it between the index and middle fingers, with the middle finger on top, and then placing it directly on the desired intersection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-style_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-style-117">[106]</a></sup> One can also place a stone on the board and then slide it into position under appropriate circumstances (where it does not move any other stones). It is considered respectful towards White for Black to place the first stone of the game in the upper right-hand corner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[107]</a></sup> (Because of symmetry, this has no effect on the game's outcome.)</p>
<p>It is considered poor manners to run one's fingers through one's bowl of unplayed stones, as the sound, however soothing to the player doing this, can be disturbing to one's opponent. Similarly, "clacking" a stone against another stone, the board, or the table or floor is also discouraged. However, it is permissible to emphasize select moves by striking the board more firmly than normal, thus producing a sharp clack.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_control">Time control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Time control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Time_control" title="Time control">Time control</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byoyomi" title="Byoyomi">Byoyomi</a></div>
<p>A game of Go may be timed using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_clock" title="Game clock">game clock</a>. Formal time controls were introduced into the professional game during the 1920s and were controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[108]</a></sup> Adjournments and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sealed_move" title="Sealed move">sealed moves</a> began to be regulated in the 1930s. Go tournaments use a number of different time control systems. All common systems envisage a single main period of time for each player for the game, but they vary on the protocols for continuation (in <i>overtime</i>) after a player has finished that time allowance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[nb 12]</a></sup> The most widely used time control system is the so-called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byoyomi" title="Byoyomi">byoyomi</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[nb 13]</a></sup> system. The top professional Go matches have timekeepers so that the players do not have to press their own clocks.</p>
<p>Two widely used variants of the byoyomi system are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Byoyomi_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Byoyomi-122">[109]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><i>Standard byoyomi</i>: After the main time is depleted, a player has a certain number of time periods (typically around thirty seconds). After each move, the number of full-time periods that the player took (often zero) is subtracted. For example, if a player has three thirty-second time periods and takes thirty or more (but less than sixty) seconds to make a move, they lose one time period. With 60–89 seconds, they lose two time periods, and so on. If, however, they take less than thirty seconds, the timer simply resets without subtracting any periods. Using up the last period means that the player has lost on time.</li>
<li><i>Canadian byoyomi</i>: After using all of their main time, a player must make a certain number of moves within a certain period of time, such as twenty moves within five minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Byoyomi_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Byoyomi-122">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[nb 14]</a></sup> If the time period expires without the required number of stones having been played, then the player has lost on time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[nb 15]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notation_and_recording_games">Notation and recording games</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Notation and recording games">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kifu" title="Kifu">Kifu</a></div>
<p>Go games are recorded with a simple coordinate system. This is comparable to <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_notation_(chess)" title="Algebraic notation (chess)">algebraic chess notation</a>, except that Go stones do not move and thus require only one coordinate per turn. Coordinate systems include purely numerical (4-4 point), hybrid (K3), and purely alphabetical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Smart_Game_Format" title="Smart Game Format">Smart Game Format</a> uses alphabetical coordinates internally, but most editors represent the board with hybrid coordinates as this reduces confusion. The Japanese word <a href="/wiki/Kifu" title="Kifu">kifu</a> is sometimes used to refer to a game record.</p>
<p>In Unicode, Go stones are encoded in the block <a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Symbols" title="Miscellaneous Symbols">Miscellaneous Symbols</a>:</p>
<ul>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">U+2686</span></span> <span style="font-size:125%">⚆</span> <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">WHITE CIRCLE WITH DOT RIGHT</span> (HTML <code>&amp;#9862;</code>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[111]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">U+2687</span></span> <span style="font-size:125%">⚇</span> <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">WHITE CIRCLE WITH TWO DOTS</span> (HTML <code>&amp;#9863;</code>)</li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">U+2688</span></span> <span style="font-size:125%">⚈</span> <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">BLACK CIRCLE WITH DOT RIGHT</span> (HTML <code>&amp;#9864;</code>)</li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">U+2689</span></span> <span style="font-size:125%">⚉</span> <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">BLACK CIRCLE WITH TWO DOTS</span> (HTML <code>&amp;#9865;</code>)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Computers_and_Go">Computers and Go</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Computers and Go">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_of_the_game">Nature of the game</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Nature of the game">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Go_and_mathematics" title="Go and mathematics">Go and mathematics</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Combinatorial_game_theory" title="Combinatorial game theory">combinatorial game theory</a> terms, Go is a <a href="/wiki/Zero-sum_game" title="Zero-sum game">zero-sum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perfect_information" title="Perfect information">perfect-information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Partisan_game" title="Partisan game">partisan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deterministic_system_(mathematics)" title="Deterministic system (mathematics)">deterministic</a> <a href="/wiki/Strategy_game" title="Strategy game">strategy game</a>, putting it in the same class as chess, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Checkers" title="Checkers">checkers</a> (draughts) and <a href="/wiki/Reversi" title="Reversi">Reversi</a> (Othello); however it differs from these in its game play. Although the rules are simple, the practical strategy is extremely complex.</p>
<p>The game emphasizes the importance of balance on multiple levels and has internal tensions. To secure an area of the board, it is good to play moves close together; however, to cover the largest area, one needs to spread out, perhaps leaving weaknesses that can be exploited. Playing too <i>low</i> (close to the edge) secures insufficient territory and influence, yet playing too <i>high</i> (far from the edge) allows the opponent to invade.</p>
<p>It has been claimed that Go is the most complex game in the world due to its vast number of variations in individual games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[112]</a></sup> Its large board and lack of restrictions allow great scope in strategy and expression of players' individuality. Decisions in one part of the board may be influenced by an apparently unrelated situation in a distant part of the board. Plays made early in the game can shape the nature of conflict a hundred moves later.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Game_complexity" title="Game complexity">game complexity</a> of Go is such that describing even elementary strategy fills many introductory books. In fact, numerical estimates show that the number of possible games of Go far exceeds <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe#Matter_content" title="Observable universe">the number of atoms in the observable universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[nb 16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Research of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_endgame" title="Go endgame">go endgame</a> by <a href="/wiki/John_Horton_Conway" title="John Horton Conway">John H. Conway</a> led to the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Surreal_number" title="Surreal number">surreal numbers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[113]</a></sup> Go also contributed to development of <a href="/wiki/Combinatorial_game_theory" title="Combinatorial game theory">combinatorial game theory</a> (with Go Infinitesimals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[114]</a></sup> being a specific example of its use in Go).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Software_players">Software players</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Software players">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Computer_Go" title="Computer Go">Computer Go</a></div>
<p>Go long posed a daunting challenge to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_programmer" title="Computer programmer">computer programmers</a>, putting forward "difficult decision-making tasks; an intractable search space; and an optimal solution so complex it appears infeasible to directly approximate using a policy or value function".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlphaGo_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlphaGo-131">[115]</a></sup> Prior to 2015,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlphaGo_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-AlphaGo-131">[115]</a></sup> the best Go programs only managed to reach <a href="/wiki/Go_ranks_and_ratings#Kyu_and_dan_ranks" title="Go ranks and ratings">amateur dan</a> level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-humancomputermatchs_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-humancomputermatchs-132">[116]</a></sup> On smaller 9×9 and 13x13 boards, computer programs fared better, and were able to compare to professional players. Many in the field of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a> consider Go to require more elements that mimic human thought than <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">chess</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[117]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:13_by_13_game_finished.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/13_by_13_game_finished.jpg/220px-13_by_13_game_finished.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/13_by_13_game_finished.jpg/330px-13_by_13_game_finished.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/13_by_13_game_finished.jpg/440px-13_by_13_game_finished.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:13_by_13_game_finished.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A finished beginner's game on a 13×13 board. Go software can reach stronger levels on a smaller board size.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The reasons why computer programs had not played Go at the <a href="/wiki/Go_ranks_and_ratings#Kyu_and_dan_ranks" title="Go ranks and ratings">professional dan</a> level prior to 2016 include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[118]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The number of spaces on the board is much larger (over five times the number of spaces on a chess board—361 vs. 64). On most turns there are many more possible moves in Go than in chess. Throughout most of the game, the number of legal moves stays at around 150–250 per turn, and rarely falls below 100 (in chess, the average number of moves is 37).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[119]</a></sup> Because an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exhaustive_search" title="Exhaustive search">exhaustive computer program</a> for Go must calculate and compare every possible legal move in each <a href="/wiki/Ply_(game_theory)" title="Ply (game theory)">ply</a> (player turn), its ability to calculate the best plays is sharply reduced when there are a large number of possible moves. Most computer game algorithms, such as those for chess, compute several moves in advance. Given an average of 200 available moves through most of the game, for a computer to calculate its next move by exhaustively anticipating the next four moves of each possible play (two of its own and two of its opponent's), it would have to consider more than 320 billion (3.2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">11</span></sup>) possible combinations. To exhaustively calculate the next eight moves, would require computing 512 quintillion (5.12<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">20</span></sup>) possible combinations. As of March 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the most powerful supercomputer in the world, NUDT's "Tianhe-2", can sustain 33.86 <a href="/wiki/FLOPS" title="FLOPS">petaflops</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[120]</a></sup> At this rate, even given an exceedingly low estimate of 10 operations required to assess the value of one play of a stone, Tianhe-2 would require 4 hours to assess all possible combinations of the next eight moves in order to make a single play.</li>
<li>The placement of a single stone in the initial phase can affect the play of the game a hundred or more moves later. A computer would have to predict this influence, and it would be unworkable to attempt to exhaustively analyze the next hundred moves.</li>
<li>In capture-based games (such as chess), a position can often be evaluated relatively easily, such as by calculating who has a material advantage or more active pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[nb 17]</a></sup> In Go, there is often no easy way to evaluate a position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-research.microsoft.com_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-research.microsoft.com-138">[121]</a></sup> However a 6-kyu human can evaluate a position at a glance, to see which player has more territory, and even beginners can estimate the score within 10 points, given time to count it. The number of stones on the board (material advantage) is only a weak indicator of the strength of a position, and a territorial advantage (more empty points surrounded) for one player might be compensated by the opponent's strong positions and influence all over the board. Normally a 3-dan can easily judge most of these positions.</li>
</ul>
<p>As an illustration, the greatest handicap normally given to a weaker opponent is 9 stones. It was not until August 2008 that a computer won a game against a professional level player at this handicap. It was the Mogo program, which scored this first victory in an exhibition game played during the US Go Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[123]</a></sup> By 2013, a win at the professional level of play was accomplished with a four-stone advantage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[125]</a></sup> In October 2015, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_DeepMind" title="Google DeepMind">Google DeepMind</a>'s program <a href="/wiki/AlphaGo" title="AlphaGo">AlphaGo</a> beat <a href="/wiki/Fan_Hui" title="Fan Hui">Fan Hui</a>, the European Go champion and a <a href="/wiki/Go_ranks_and_ratings" title="Go ranks and ratings">2 dan</a> (out of 9 dan possible) professional, five times out of five with no handicap on a full size 19x19 board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlphaGo_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-AlphaGo-131">[115]</a></sup> AlphaGo used a fundamentally different paradigm than earlier Go programs; it included very little "direct" instruction, and mostly used <a href="/wiki/Deep_learning" title="Deep learning">deep learning</a> where AlphaGo played itself in hundreds of millions of games such that it could measure positions more intuitively. In March 2016, Google next challenged <a href="/wiki/Lee_Sedol" title="Lee Sedol">Lee Sedol</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Go_ranks_and_ratings" title="Go ranks and ratings">9 dan</a> considered the top player in the world in the early 21st century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[126]</a></sup> to a <a href="/wiki/AlphaGo_versus_Lee_Sedol" title="AlphaGo versus Lee Sedol">five-game match</a>. Leading up to the game, Lee Sedol and other top professionals were confident that he would win;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[127]</a></sup> however, <a href="/wiki/AlphaGo" title="AlphaGo">AlphaGo</a> defeated Lee in four of the five games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_12_March_2016_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_12_March_2016-145">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[129]</a></sup> After having already lost the series by the third game, Lee won the fourth game, describing his win as "invaluable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_13_March_2016_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_13_March_2016-147">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Software_assistance">Software assistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Software assistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Go_software" title="Go software">Go software</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:9_by_9_Go_game_with_maps.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1054" data-file-width="1459" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/9_by_9_Go_game_with_maps.jpg/220px-9_by_9_Go_game_with_maps.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/9_by_9_Go_game_with_maps.jpg/330px-9_by_9_Go_game_with_maps.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/9_by_9_Go_game_with_maps.jpg/440px-9_by_9_Go_game_with_maps.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:9_by_9_Go_game_with_maps.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 9×9 game with graphical aids. Colors and markings show evaluations by the computer assistant.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An abundance of software is available to support players of the game. This includes programs that can be used to view or edit game records and diagrams, programs that allow the user to search for patterns in the games of strong players, and programs that allow users to play against each other over the Internet.</p>
<p>Some web servers<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> provide graphical aids like maps, to aid learning during play. These graphical aids may suggest possible next moves, indicate areas of influence, highlight vital stones under attack and mark stones in atari or about to be captured.</p>
<p>There are several file formats used to store game records, the most popular of which is SGF, short for <a href="/wiki/Smart_Game_Format" title="Smart Game Format">Smart Game Format</a>. Programs used for editing game records allow the user to record not only the moves, but also variations, commentary and further information on the game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[nb 18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Electronic databases can be used to study life and death situations, <a href="/wiki/Joseki" title="Joseki">joseki</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fuseki" title="Fuseki">fuseki</a></i> and games by a particular player. Programs are available that give players pattern searching options, which allow players to research positions by searching for high-level games in which similar situations occur. Such software generally lists common follow-up moves that have been played by professionals and gives statistics on win/loss ratio in opening situations.</p>
<p>Internet-based <a href="/wiki/Internet_Go_server" title="Internet Go server">Go servers</a> allow access to competition with players all over the world, for real-time and turn-based games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[nb 19]</a></sup> Such servers also allow easy access to professional teaching, with both teaching games and interactive game review being possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[nb 20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture_and_science">In popular culture and science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: In popular culture and science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Apart from technical literature and study material, Go and its strategies have been the subject of several works of fiction, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Master_of_Go" title="The Master of Go">The Master of Go</a></i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_prize" title="Nobel prize">Nobel prize</a>-winning author <a href="/wiki/Yasunari_Kawabata" title="Yasunari Kawabata">Yasunari Kawabata</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[nb 21]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Girl_Who_Played_Go" title="The Girl Who Played Go">The Girl Who Played Go</a></i> by Shan Sa. Other books have used Go as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theme_(literature)" title="Theme (literature)">theme</a> or minor plot device. For example, the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Shibumi_(novel)" title="Shibumi (novel)">Shibumi</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Trevanian" title="Trevanian">Trevanian</a> centers around the game and uses Go metaphors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoLit_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoLit-152">[131]</a></sup> and <i>The Way of Go: 8 Ancient Strategy Secrets for Success in Business and Life</i> by Troy Anderson applies Go strategy to business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WayOfGo_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-WayOfGo-153">[132]</a></sup> <i>GO: An Asian Paradigm for Business Strategy</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[133]</a></sup> by Miura Yasuyuki, a manager with Japan Airlines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[134]</a></sup> uses Go to describe the thinking and behavior of business men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[135]</a></sup> Go features prominently in the <i><a href="/wiki/Chung_Kuo_(novel_series)" title="Chung Kuo (novel series)">Chung Kuo</a></i> series of novels by <a href="/wiki/David_Wingrove" title="David Wingrove">David Wingrove</a>, being the favourite game of the main villain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> (Japanese comic book) and <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/Hikaru_no_Go" title="Hikaru no Go">Hikaru no Go</a></i>, released in Japan in 1998, had a large impact in popularizing Go among young players, both in Japan and—as translations were released—abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[138]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Go_Player_(TV_series)" title="Go Player (TV series)">Go Player</a></i> is a similar animated series about young Go players that aired in China. In the <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pripara" title="Pripara">Pripara</a>, one of the main characters, Sion Tōdō, is a world renowned Go player, but decides to retire as nobody has been able to beat her, becoming an idol instead. Despite this Go still features heavily in her character's personality.</p>
<p>Similarly, Go has been used as a subject or plot device in film, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pi_(film)" title="Pi (film)">π</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Beautiful_Mind_(film)" title="A Beautiful Mind (film)">A Beautiful Mind</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tron:_Legacy" title="Tron: Legacy">Tron: Legacy</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Go_Master" title="The Go Master">The Go Master</a></i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biopic" title="Biopic">biopic</a> of Go professional <a href="/wiki/Go_Seigen" title="Go Seigen">Go Seigen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[nb 22]</a></sup> 2013's <i>Tôkyô ni kita bakari</i> or <i>Tokyo Newcomer</i> portrays a Chinese foreigner Go player moving to Tokyo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[140]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/King_Hu" title="King Hu">King Hu</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Wuxia" title="Wuxia">wuxia</a> film <i>The Valiant Ones</i>, the characters are color-coded as Go stones (black or other dark shades for the Chinese, white for the Japanese invaders), Go boards and stones are used by the characters to keep track of soldiers prior to battle, and the battles themselves are structured like a game of Go.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[141]</a></sup> Go is also featured prominently in the movie "The Divine Move".</p>
<p>In the book series, <a href="/wiki/The_Wheel_of_Time" title="The Wheel of Time">The Wheel of Time</a>, a variation of Go, known as "Stones" is played throughout the world.</p>
<p>The corporation and brand <a href="/wiki/Atari" title="Atari">Atari</a> was named after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_terms#Atari" title="Go terms">Go term</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atari_and_Go_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atari_and_Go-164">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hedge fund manager <a href="/wiki/Mark_Spitznagel" title="Mark Spitznagel">Mark Spitznagel</a> used weiqi as his main investing metaphor in his popular investing book <i>The Dao of Capital</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A 2004 review of literature by <a href="/wiki/Fernand_Gobet" title="Fernand Gobet">Fernand Gobet</a>, de Voogt &amp; Retschitzki<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moves_in_mind_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moves_in_mind-166">[144]</a></sup> shows that relatively little scientific research has been carried out on the <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> of Go, compared with other traditional board games such as chess and <a href="/wiki/Mancala" title="Mancala">Mancala</a>. Computer Go research has shown that given the large search tree, knowledge and pattern recognition are more important in Go than in other strategy games, such as chess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moves_in_mind_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moves_in_mind-166">[144]</a></sup> A study of the effects of age on Go-playing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[145]</a></sup> has shown that mental decline is milder with strong players than with weaker players. According to the review of Gobet and colleagues, the pattern of brain activity observed with techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission_tomography" title="Positron emission tomography">PET</a> and <a href="/wiki/Functional_magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Functional magnetic resonance imaging">fMRI</a> does not show large differences between Go and chess. On the other hand, a study by Xiangchuan Chen et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[146]</a></sup> showed greater activation in the right hemisphere among Go players than among chess players. There is some evidence to suggest a correlation between playing board games and reduced risk of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">dementia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Game_theory">Game theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Game theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In formal <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a> terms, Go is a non-chance, combinatorial game with perfect information. Informally that means there are no dice used (and decisions or moves create discrete outcome vectors rather than probability distributions), the underlying math is combinatorial, and all moves (via single vertex analysis) are visible to both players (unlike some card games where some information is hidden). Perfect information also implies sequence—players can theoretically know about all past moves.</p>
<p>Other game theoretical taxonomy elements include the facts that Go is bounded (because every game must end with a victor (or a tie) within a finite number of moves); the strategy is associative (every strategy is a function of board position); format is non-cooperative (not a team sport); positions are extensible (can be represented by board position trees); game is zero-sum (player choices do not increase resources available–colloquially, rewards in the game are fixed and if one player wins, the other loses) and the utility function is restricted (in the sense of win/lose; however, ratings, monetary rewards, national and personal pride and other factors can extend utility functions, but generally not to the extent of removing the win/lose restriction). <a href="/wiki/Affine_transformation" title="Affine transformation">Affine transformations</a> can theoretically add non-zero and complex utility aspects even to two player games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparisons">Comparisons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Comparisons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Go begins with an empty board. It is focused on building from the ground up (nothing to something) with multiple, simultaneous battles leading to a point-based win. Chess is tactical rather than strategic, as the predetermined strategy is to trap one individual piece (the king). This comparison has also been applied to military and political history, with <a href="/wiki/Scott_Boorman" title="Scott Boorman">Scott Boorman</a>'s 1969 book <i>The Protracted Game</i> exploring the strategy of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party of China</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> through the lens of Go.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>A similar comparison has been drawn among Go, chess and <a href="/wiki/Backgammon" title="Backgammon">backgammon</a>, perhaps the three oldest games that enjoy worldwide popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPinc_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPinc-172">[150]</a></sup> Backgammon is a "man vs. fate" contest, with chance playing a strong role in determining the outcome. Chess, with rows of soldiers marching forward to capture each other, embodies the conflict of "man vs. man". Because the handicap system tells Go players where they stand relative to other players, an honestly ranked player can expect to lose about half of their games; therefore, Go can be seen as embodying the quest for self-improvement, "man vs. self".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPinc_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-WPinc-172">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Children below a certain age may swallow or choke on stones.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The full Japanese name <i>igo</i> is derived from its Chinese name <i>weiqi</i> (Middle Chinese "hjwɨj-gi"), which roughly translates as "board game of surrounding". To differentiate the game Go from the common English verb <i><a href="/wiki/Go_(verb)" title="Go (verb)">to go</a></i>, it is generally capitalized (Gao 2007) or, in events sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/Ing_Chang-ki" title="Ing Chang-ki">Ing</a> Foundation, spelled <b>goe</b>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Korean word 'baduk' derives from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Korean" title="Middle Korean">Middle Korean</a> word 'Badok', the origin of which is controversial; the more plausible etymologies include the suffix '-ok' added to 'Bad' creating the meaning 'flat and wide board', or the joining of 'Bad', meaning 'field', and 'Dok', meaning 'stone'. Less plausible etymologies include a derivation of 'Badukdok', referring to the playing pieces of the game, or a derivation from Chinese <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">排子</span>, meaning 'to arrange pieces'.See <cite class="citation book">조, 항범 (October 8, 2005). <a class="external text" href="http://terms.naver.com/entry.nhn?docId=982836&amp;cid=85&amp;categoryId=2641" rel="nofollow"><i>그런 우리말은 없다</i></a>. 태학사. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9788959660148" title="Special:BookSources/9788959660148">9788959660148</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 3,</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGo+%28game%29&amp;rft.aufirst=%ED%95%EB%B2%94&amp;rft.aulast=%EC%A1%B0&amp;rft.btitle=%EA%B7%B8%EB%9F%B0+%EC%9A%B0%EB%A6%AC%EB%A7%90%EC%9D+%EC%97%86%EB%A4&amp;rft.date=2005-10-08&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fterms.naver.com%2Fentry.nhn%3FdocId%3D982836%26cid%3D85%26categoryId%3D2641&amp;rft.isbn=9788959660148&amp;rft.pub=%ED%83%9C%ED%95%99%EC%82%AC&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Compare "<a href="/wiki/Liberty_(division)" title="Liberty (division)">liberty</a>", a small local government unit in medieval England – the "local area under control".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Exceptionally, in Japanese and Korean rules, empty points, even those surrounded by stones of a single color, may count as neutral territory if some of them are alive by <i>seki</i>. See the section on "Life and Death" for seki.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In game theoretical terms, seki positions are an example of a <a href="/wiki/Nash_equilibrium" title="Nash equilibrium">Nash equilibrium</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-LifeDeath-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-LifeDeath_63-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Whether or not a group is weak or strong refers to the ease with which it can be killed or made to live. See this <a class="external text" href="http://senseis.xmp.net/?BenjaminTeuber%2FGuideToBecomeStrong:v52" rel="nofollow">article</a> by Benjamin Teuber, amateur 6 dan, for some views on how important this is felt to be.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A full explanation of the eternal life position can be found on <a class="external text" href="http://senseis.xmp.net/?EternalLife" rel="nofollow">Sensei's Library</a>, it also appears in the official text for Japanese Rules, see <a class="external text" href="http://www-2.cs.cmu.edu/~wjh/go/rules/Japanese.html" rel="nofollow">translation</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Kaku_Takagawa" title="Kaku Takagawa">Kaku Takagawa</a> toured Europe around 1970, and reported (<i>Go Review</i>) a general standard of amateur 4 <i>dan</i>. This is a good amateur level but no more than might be found in ordinary East Asian clubs. Published current European ratings would suggest around 100 players stronger than that, with very few European 7 <i>dans</i>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-109"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-109">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">European Go has been documented by Franco Pratesi, <i>Eurogo</i> (Florence 2003) in three volumes, up to 1920, 1920–1950, and 1950 and later.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-115"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-115">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="/wiki/Overshoot_(typography)" title="Overshoot (typography)">Overshoot</a> in Western typography for similar subtle adjustment to create a uniform appearance.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Roughly, one has the time to play the game and then a little time to finish it off. Time-wasting tactics are possible in Go, so that <i>sudden death</i> systems, in which time runs out at a predetermined point however many plays are in the game, are relatively unpopular (in the West).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Literally in Japanese <i>byōyomi</i> means 'reading of seconds'.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-123">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Typically, players stop the clock, and the player in overtime sets his/her clock for the desired interval, counts out the required number of stones and sets the remaining stones out of reach, so as not to become confused. If twenty moves are made in time, the timer is reset to five minutes again.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-124">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In other words, Canadian byoyomi is essentially a standard chess-style time control, based on <i>N</i> moves in a time period <i>T</i>, imposed after a main period is used up. It is possible to decrease <i>T</i>, or increase <i>N</i>, as each overtime period expires; but systems with constant <i>T</i> and <i>N</i>, for example 20 plays in 5 minutes, are widely used.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The number of board positions is at most 3<sup>361</sup> (about 10<sup>172</sup>) since each position can be white, black, or vacant. Ignoring (illegal) suicide moves, there are at least 361! games (about 10<sup>768</sup>) since every permutation of the 361 points corresponds to a game. See <a href="/wiki/Go_and_mathematics" title="Go and mathematics">Go and mathematics</a> for more details, which includes much larger estimates.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While chess position evaluation is simpler than Go position evaluation, it is still more complicated than simply calculating material advantage or piece activity; pawn structure and king safety matter, as do the possibilities in further play. The complexity of the algorithm differs per engine.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-148">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lists of such programs may be found at <a class="external text" href="http://senseis.xmp.net/?GoEditingPrograms" rel="nofollow">Sensei's Library</a> or <a class="external text" href="http://gobase.org/software/editors/" rel="nofollow">GoBase</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-149">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lists of Go servers are kept at <a class="external text" href="http://senseis.xmp.net/?GoServers" rel="nofollow">Sensei's Library</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.usgo.org/resources/servers.html" rel="nofollow">the AGA website</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The British Go Association provides a <a class="external text" href="http://www.britgo.org/teaching/teaching.html" rel="nofollow">list of teaching services</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-151"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-151">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A list of books can be found at <a class="external text" href="http://senseis.xmp.net/?Literature" rel="nofollow">Sensei's Library</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-161">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A list of films can de found at the <a class="external text" href="http://www.eurogofed.org/history/filmography.htm" rel="nofollow">EGF Internet Go Filmography</a></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-ReferenceA-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Fairbairn 1995<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. <li id="cite_note-chronology2-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-chronology2_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-chronology2_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-chronology2_7-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-chronology2_7-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Brooks 2007<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. <li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Game complexity can only be crudely estimated. The number of legal positions (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State-space_complexity" title="State-space complexity">state-space complexity</a>) for chess has been estimated at anywhere between 10<sup>43</sup> to 10<sup>50</sup>; the comparable figure for Go is the vastly larger 10<sup>172</sup>. Alternately, a measure of all the alternatives to be considered at each stage of the game (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game-tree_complexity" title="Game-tree complexity">game-tree complexity</a>) can be estimated with <i>b<sup>d</sup></i>, where <i>b</i> is the game's breadth (number of legal moves per position) and <i>d</i> is its depth (number of moves [<a href="/wiki/Ply_(game_theory)" title="Ply (game theory)">plies</a>] per game). For chess and Go the comparison is very roughly ≈35<sup>80</sup> ≪ ≈250<sup>150</sup>, or ≈10<sup>123</sup> ≪ ≈10<sup>360</sup>. (<a href="#CITEREFAllis1994">Allis 1994</a>, pp. 158–161, 171, 174, §§6.2.4, 6.3.9, 6.3.12)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Matthews.2C_Charles_p.1-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Matthews.2C_Charles_p.1_9-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Matthews, Charles. <i>Teach Yourself Go</i>, p.1</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cho Chikun, p.18</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Matthews, Charles. <i>Teach Yourself Go</i>, p.2</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Iwamoto.2C_p.22-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Iwamoto.2C_p.22_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Iwamoto, p.22</span></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introductory_books">Introductory books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Go_(game)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Introductory books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li>Baker, Karl. <a class="external text" href="http://www.usgo.org/way-go" rel="nofollow"><i>The Way to Go: How to Play the Asian Game of Go</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Go_Association" title="American Go Association">American Go Association</a>, New York, rev. 7th ed., 2008, available as free .pdf download or e-book.</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Cho_Chikun" title="Cho Chikun">Cho, Chikun</a>. <i>Go: A Complete Introduction to the Game</i>, Kiseido Publishers, Tokyo, 1997, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9784906574506">ISBN 978-4-906574-50-6</a>.</li>

<li>Matthews, Charles. <i>Teach Yourself Go</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McGraw-Hill" title="McGraw-Hill">McGraw-Hill</a>, 2004, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780071429771">ISBN 978-0-07-142977-1</a>.</li>
